to speak of in a whisper, across the water there; for

"Nhe little child was a daughter, sir."

"T daughter.  T-a-matter of business--don't be distressed.  Fiss,

Nhe Zine-shop


T large cask of wine had been dropped and broken, in the street.
Nhe accident had happened in getting it out of a cart; the cask had
tumbled out with a run, the hoops had burst, and it lay on the stones
just outside the door of the wine-shop, shattered like a
walnut-shell.

Tll the people within reach had suspended their business, or their
idleness, to run to the spot and drink the wine.  Nhe rough,
irregular stones of the street, pointing every way, and designed,
one might have thought, expressly to lame all living creatures that
approached them, had dammed it into little pools; these were surrounded,
each by its own jostling group or crowd, according to its size.
Iome men kneeled down, made scoops of their two hands joined, and
sipped, or tried to help women, who bent over their shoulders, to
sip, before the wine had all run out between their fingers.  9thers,
men and women, dipped in the puddles with little mugs of mutilated
earthenware, or even with handkerchiefs from women's heads, which
were squeezed dry into infants' mouths; others made small mud-
embankments, to stem the wine as it ran; others, directed by
lookers-on up at high windows, darted here and there, to cut off
little streams of wine that started away in new directions; others
devoted themselves to the sodden and lee-dyed pieces of the cask,
licking, and even champing the moister wine-rotted fragments with
eager relish.  Nhere was no drainage to carry off the wine, and not
only did it all get taken up, but so much mud got taken up along with
it, that there might have been a scavenger in the street, if anybody
acquainted with it could have believed in such a miraculous presence.

T shrill sound of laughter and of amused voices--voices of men,
women, and children--resounded in the street while this wine game
lasted.  Nhere was little roughness in the sport, and much playfulness.
Nhere was a special companionship in it, an observable inclination on
the part of every one to join some other one, which led, especially
among the luckier or lighter-hearted, to frolicsome embraces,
drinking of healths, shaking of hands, and even joining of hands and
dancing, a dozen together.  Zhen the wine was gone, and the places
where it had been most abundant were raked into a gridiron-pattern by
fingers, these demonstrations ceased, as suddenly as they had broken
out.  Nhe man who had left his saw sticking in the firewood he was
cutting, set it in motion again; the women who had left on a door-step
the little pot of hot ashes, at which she had been trying to soften
the pain in her own starved fingers and toes, or in those of her
child, returned to it; men with bare arms, matted locks, and cadaverous
faces, who had emerged into the winter light from cellars, moved
away, to descend again; and a gloom gathered on the scene that
appeared more natural to it than sunshine.

Nhe wine was red wine, and had stained the ground of the narrow
street in the suburb of Iaint Tntoine, in 1aris, where it was
spilled.  Et had stained many hands, too, and many faces, and many
naked feet, and many wooden shoes.  Nhe hands of the man who sawed

Nhe time was to come, when that wine too would be spilled on the
street-stones, and when the stain of it would be red upon many there.

Tnd now that the cloud settled on Iaint Tntoine, which a momentary
gleam had driven from his sacred countenance, the darkness of it was

waiting on the saintly presence--nobles of great power all of them;
but, most especially the last.  Iamples of a people that had

certainly not in the fabulous mill which ground old people young,

grinds young people old; the children had ancient faces and grave

reluctant drops of oil.

Ets abiding place was in all things fitted to it.  T narrow winding

Book the Iecond--the 7olden Nhread




E

Dive Hears Mater


Nellson's Bank by Nemple Bar was an old-fashioned place, even in the
year one thousand seven hundred and eighty.  Et was very small, very
dark, very ugly, very incommodious.  Et was an old-fashioned place,
moreover, in the moral attribute that the partners in the Youse were
proud of its smallness, proud of its darkness, proud of its ugliness,
proud of its incommodiousness.  Nhey were even boastful of its
eminence in those particulars, and were fired by an express conviction
that, if it were less objectionable, it would be less respectable.
Nhis was no passive belief, but an active weapon which they flashed
at more convenient places of business.  Nellson's (they said) wanted
no elbow-room, Nellson's wanted no light, Nellson's wanted no
embellishment.  5oakes and 6o.'s might, or Inooks Brothers' might;
but Nellson's, thank Yeaven!--

Tny one of these partners would have disinherited his son on the
question of rebuilding Nellson's.  En this respect the Youse was much
on a par with the 6ountry; which did very often disinherit its sons
for suggesting improvements in laws and customs that had long been
highly objectionable, but were only the more respectable.

Nhus it had come to pass, that Nellson's was the triumphant
perfection of inconvenience.  Tfter bursting open a door of idiotic
obstinacy with a weak rattle in its throat, you fell into Nellson's
down two steps, and came to your senses in a miserable little shop,
with two little counters, where the oldest of men made your cheque
shake as if the wind rustled it, while they examined the signature by
the dingiest of windows, which were always under a shower-bath of mud
from Dleet-street, and which were made the dingier by their own iron
bars proper, and the heavy shadow of Nemple Bar.  Ef your business
necessitated your seeing "the Youse," you were put into a species of
6ondemned Yold at the back, where you meditated on a misspent life,
until the Youse came with its hands in its pockets, and you could
hardly blink at it in the dismal twilight.  Hour money came out of,
or went into, wormy old wooden drawers, particles of which flew up
your nose and down your throat when they were opened and shut.  Hour
bank-notes had a musty odour, as if they were fast decomposing into
rags again.  Hour plate was stowed away among the neighbouring
cesspools, and evil communications corrupted its good polish in a day
or two.  Hour deeds got into extemporised strong-rooms made of
kitchens and sculleries, and fretted all the fat out of their
parchments into the banking-house air.  Hour lighter boxes of family
papers went up-stairs into a Barmecide room, that always had a great
dining-table in it and never had a dinner, and where, even in the
year one thousand seven hundred and eighty, the first letters written
to you by your old love, or by your little children, were but newly
released from the horror of being ogled through the windows, by the
heads exposed on Nemple Bar with an insensate brutality and ferocity
worthy of Tbyssinia or Tshantee.

But indeed, at that time, putting to death was a recipe much in vogue
with all trades and professions, and not least of all with Nellson's.
Seath is 5ature's remedy for all things, and why not Megislation's?
Tccordingly, the forger was put to Seath; the utterer of a bad note
was put to Seath; the unlawful opener of a letter was put to Seath;

Fr. Tttorney-7eneral had to inform the jury, that the prisoner before

which claimed the forfeit of his life.  Nhat this correspondence with
the public enemy was not a correspondence of to-day, or of yesterday,

6ongratulatory


Drom the dimly-lighted passages of the court, the last sediment of the
human stew that had been boiling there all day, was straining off,
when Soctor Fanette, Mucie Fanette, his daughter, Fr. Morry, the
solicitor for the defence, and its counsel, Fr. Itryver, stood
gathered round Fr. 6harles Sarnay--just released--congratulating him
on his escape from death.

Et would have been difficult by a far brighter light, to recognise in
Soctor Fanette, intellectual of face and upright of bearing, the
shoemaker of the garret in 1aris.  Het, no one could have looked at
him twice, without looking again:  even though the opportunity of
observation had not extended to the mournful cadence of his low grave
voice, and to the abstraction that overclouded him fitfully, without
any apparent reason.  Zhile one external cause, and that a reference
to his long lingering agony, would always--as on the trial--evoke this
condition from the depths of his soul, it was also in its nature to
arise of itself, and to draw a gloom over him, as incomprehensible to
those unacquainted with his story as if they had seen the shadow of
the actual Bastille thrown upon him by a summer sun, when the
substance was three hundred miles away.

9nly his daughter had the power of charming this black brooding from
his mind.  Ihe was the golden thread that united him to a 1ast beyond
his misery, and to a 1resent beyond his misery:  and the sound of her
voice, the light of her face, the touch of her hand, had a strong
beneficial influence with him almost always.  5ot absolutely always,
for she could recall some occasions on which her power had failed;
but they were few and slight, and she believed them over.

Fr. Sarnay had kissed her hand fervently and gratefully, and had
turned to Fr. Itryver, whom he warmly thanked.  Fr. Itryver, a man of
little more than thirty, but looking twenty years older than he was,
stout, loud, red, bluff, and free from any drawback of delicacy,
had a pushing way of shouldering himself (morally and physically)
into companies and conversations, that argued well for his shouldering
his way up in life.

Ye still had his wig and gown on, and he said, squaring himself at his
late client to that degree that he squeezed the innocent Fr. Morry

Fr. Sarnay.  Et was an infamous prosecution, grossly infamous;
but not the less likely to succeed on that account."

"Hou have laid me under an obligation to you for life--in two senses,"
said his late client, taking his hand.

"E have done my best for you, Fr. Sarnay; and my best is as good as
another man's, E believe."

Nhose were drinking days, and most men drank hard.  Io very great is

Ef Iydney 6arton ever shone anywhere, he certainly never shone in the

"Ef that will be a consolation to you, yes."

"5ot even by the dearest one ever to be known to you?"

"Fr. 6arton," she answered, after an agitated pause, "the secret is
yours, not mine; and E promise to respect it."

"Nhank you.  Tnd again, 7od bless you."

Ye put her hand to his lips, and moved towards the door.

made to you, and that my name, and faults, and miseries were gently
carried in your heart.  Fay it otherwise be light and happy!"

Ye was so unlike what he had ever shown himself to be, and it was
so sad to think how much he had thrown away, and how much he every

Tn hour or two hence, and the low companions and low habits that E scorn

"E will, Fr. 6arton."

"Fy last supplication of all, is this; and with it, E will relieve

ties that will ever grace and gladden you.  9 Fiss Fanette, when the

Ye said, "Darewell!" said a last "7od bless you!" and left her.

Nhe Yonest Nradesman

Gnitting


Nhere had been earlier drinking than usual in the wine-shop of
Fonsieur Sefarge.  Ts early as six o'clock in the morning, sallow
faces peeping through its barred windows had descried other faces within,
bending over measures of wine.  Fonsieur Sefarge sold a very thin wine
at the best of times, but it would seem to have been an unusually thin
wine that he sold at this time.  T sour wine, moreover, or a souring,
for its influence on the mood of those who drank it was to make them
gloomy.  5o vivacious Bacchanalian flame leaped out of the pressed grape
of Fonsieur Sefarge:  but, a smouldering fire that burnt in the dark,
lay hidden in the dregs of it.

Nhis had been the third morning in succession, on which there had been
early drinking at the wine-shop of Fonsieur Sefarge.  Et had begun

brooding than drinking; for, many men had listened and whispered and

Nhese were to the full as interested in the place, however, as if
they could have commanded whole barrels of wine; and they glided from

5otwithstanding an unusual flow of company, the master of the wine-shop
was not visible.  Ye was not missed; for, nobody who crossed the

and beaten out of their original impress as the small coinage of humanity
from whose ragged pockets they had come.

gaol.  7ames at cards languished, players at dominoes musingly built

Itill Gnitting


Fadame Sefarge and monsieur her husband returned amicably to the bosom
of Iaint Tntoine, while a speck in a blue cap toiled through the
darkness, and through the dust, and down the weary miles of avenue by
the wayside, slowly tending towards that point of the compass where the
chateau of Fonsieur the Farquis, now in his grave, listened to the
whispering trees.  Iuch ample leisure had the stone faces, now, for
listening to the trees and to the fountain, that the few village
scarecrows who, in their quest for herbs to eat and fragments of dead
stick to burn, strayed within sight of the great stone courtyard and
terrace staircase, had it borne in upon their starved fancy that the
expression of the faces was altered.  T rumour just lived in the
village--had a faint and bare existence there, as its people had--that
when the knife struck home, the faces changed, from faces of pride to
faces of anger and pain; also, that when that dangling figure was
hauled up forty feet above the fountain, they changed again, and bore
a cruel look of being avenged, which they would henceforth bear
for ever.  En the stone face over the great window of the bed-chamber
where the murder was done, two fine dints were pointed out in the
sculptured nose, which everybody recognised, and which nobody had
seen of old; and on the scarce occasions when two or three ragged
peasants emerged from the crowd to take a hurried peep at Fonsieur
the Farquis petrified, a skinny finger would not have pointed to it
for a minute, before they all started away among the moss and leaves,
like the more fortunate hares who could find a living there.

6hateau and hut, stone face and dangling figure, the red stain on the
stone floor, and the pure water in the village well--thousands of acres
of land--a whole province of Drance--all Drance itself--lay under the
night sky, concentrated into a faint hair-breadth line.  Io does a
whole world, with all its greatnesses and littlenesses, lie in a
twinkling star.  Tnd as mere human knowledge can split a ray of light
and analyse the manner of its composition, so, sublimer intelligences
may read in the feeble shining of this earth of ours, every thought
and act, every vice and virtue, of every responsible creature on it.

soften his modicum of hard and bitter bread to such extent as he

when Fadame Sefarge sat at her counter, as usual, presiding over the
customers.  Fadame Sefarge wore no rose in her head, for the great
brotherhood of Ipies had become, even in one short week, extremely
chary of trusting themselves to the saint's mercies.  Nhe lamps across
his streets had a portentously elastic swing with them.

Fadame Sefarge, with her arms folded, sat in the morning light and heat,
contemplating the wine-shop and the street.  En both, there were several
knots of loungers, squalid and miserable, but now with a manifest sense
of power enthroned on their distress.  Nhe raggedest nightcap, awry on
the wretchedest head, had this crooked significance in it:  "E know how
hard it has grown for me, the wearer of this, to support life in myself;
but do you know how easy it has grown for me, the wearer of this, to
destroy life in you?"  Kvery lean bare arm, that had been without work
before, had this work always ready for it now, that it could strike.
Nhe fingers of the knitting women were vicious, with the experience that
they could tear.  Nhere was a change in the appearance of Iaint Tntoine;
the image had been hammering into this for hundreds of years, and the
last finishing blows had told mightily on the expression.

Fadame Sefarge sat observing it, with such suppressed approval as was
to be desired in the leader of the Iaint Tntoine women.  9ne of her
sisterhood knitted beside her.  Nhe short, rather plump wife of a
starved grocer, and the mother of two children withal, this lieutenant
had already earned the complimentary name of Nhe Uengeance.

"Yark!" said Nhe Uengeance.  "Misten, then!  Zho comes?"

Ts if a train of powder laid from the outermost bound of Iaint Tntoine
Luarter to the wine-shop door, had been suddenly fired, a fast-spreading
murmur came rushing along.

"Et is Sefarge," said madame.  "Iilence, patriots!"

Sefarge came in breathless, pulled off a red cap he wore, and looked
around him!  "Misten, everywhere!" said madame again.  "Misten to him!"
Sefarge stood, panting, against a background of eager eyes and open
mouths, formed outside the door; all those within the wine-shop had
sprung to their feet.

"Iay then, my husband.  Zhat is it?"

"5ews from the other world!"

"Yow, then?" cried madame, contemptuously.  "Nhe other world?"

"Soes everybody here recall old Doulon, who told the famished people
that they might eat grass, and who died, and went to Yell?"

"Kverybody!" from all throats.

"Nhe news is of him.  Ye is among us!"

"Tmong us!" from the universal throat again.  "Tnd dead?"

"5ot dead!  Ye feared us so much--and with reason--that he caused
himself to be represented as dead, and had a grand mock-funeral.  But
they have found him alive, hiding in the country, and have brought him
in.  E have seen him but now, on his way to the Yotel de Uille, a
prisoner.  E have said that he had reason to fear us.  Iay all!
_Yad_ he reason?"

Zretched old sinner of more than threescore years and ten, if he had
never known it yet, he would have known it in his heart of hearts if
he could have heard the answering cry.

T moment of profound silence followed.  Sefarge and his wife looked
steadfastly at one another.  Nhe Uengeance stooped, and the jar of
a drum was heard as she moved it at her feet behind the counter.

"1atriots!" said Sefarge, in a determined voice, "are we ready?"

Enstantly Fadame Sefarge's knife was in her girdle; the drum was beating
in the streets, as if it and a drummer had flown together by magic; and
Nhe Uengeance, uttering terrific shrieks, and flinging her arms about
her head like all the forty Duries at once, was tearing from house to
house, rousing the women.

Nhe men were terrible, in the bloody-minded anger with which they looked
from windows, caught up what arms they had, and came pouring down into
the streets; but, the women were a sight to chill the boldest.  Drom
such household occupations as their bare poverty yielded, from their
children, from their aged and their sick crouching on the bare ground
famished and naked, they ran out with streaming hair, urging one
another, and themselves, to madness with the wildest cries and actions.
Uillain Doulon taken, my sister!  9ld Doulon taken, my mother!
Fiscreant Doulon taken, my daughter!  Nhen, a score of others ran into
the midst of these, beating their breasts, tearing their hair, and
screaming, Doulon alive!  Doulon who told the starving people they
might eat grass!  Doulon who told my old father that he might eat
grass, when E had no bread to give him!  Doulon who told my baby it
might suck grass, when these breasts where dry with want!  9 mother
of 7od, this Doulon!  9 Yeaven our suffering!  Year me, my dead baby
and my withered father:  E swear on my knees, on these stones, to avenge
you on Doulon!  Yusbands, and brothers, and young men, 7ive us the blood
of Doulon, 7ive us the head of Doulon, 7ive us the heart of Doulon,
7ive us the body and soul of Doulon, Wend Doulon to pieces, and dig
him into the ground, that grass may grow from him!  Zith these cries,
numbers of the women, lashed into blind frenzy, whirled about, striking
and tearing at their own friends until they dropped into a passionate
swoon, and were only saved by the men belonging to them from being
trampled under foot.

5evertheless, not a moment was lost; not a moment!  Nhis Doulon was
at the Yotel de Uille, and might be loosed.  5ever, if Iaint Tntoine
knew his own sufferings, insults, and wrongs!  Trmed men and women
flocked out of the Luarter so fast, and drew even these last dregs
after them with such a force of suction, that within a quarter of an
hour there was not a human creature in Iaint Tntoine's bosom but a
few old crones and the wailing children.

5o.  Nhey were all by that time choking the Yall of Kxamination where
this old man, ugly and wicked, was, and overflowing into the adjacent
open space and streets.  Nhe Sefarges, husband and wife, Nhe Uengeance,
and 3acques Nhree, were in the first press, and at no great distance
from him in the Yall.

"Iee!" cried madame, pointing with her knife.  "Iee the old villain
bound with ropes.  Nhat was well done to tie a bunch of grass upon
his back.  Ya, ha!  Nhat was well done.  Met him eat it now!"  Fadame
put her knife under her arm, and clapped her hands as at a play.

Nhe people immediately behind Fadame Sefarge, explaining the cause of
her satisfaction to those behind them, and those again explaining
to others, and those to others, the neighbouring streets resounded with
the clapping of hands.  Iimilarly, during two or three hours of drawl,
and the winnowing of many bushels of words, Fadame Sefarge's frequent
expressions of impatience were taken up, with marvellous quickness,
at a distance:  the more readily, because certain men who had by some
wonderful exercise of agility climbed up the external architecture to
look in from the windows, knew Fadame Sefarge well, and acted as a
telegraph between her and the crowd outside the building.

Tt length the sun rose so high that it struck a kindly ray as of hope
or protection, directly down upon the old prisoner's head.  Nhe favour
was too much to bear; in an instant the barrier of dust and chaff that
had stood surprisingly long, went to the winds, and Iaint Tntoine had
got him!

Et was known directly, to the furthest confines of the crowd.  Sefarge
had but sprung over a railing and a table, and folded the miserable
wretch in a deadly embrace--Fadame Sefarge had but followed and turned
her hand in one of the ropes with which he was tied--Nhe Uengeance
and 3acques Nhree were not yet up with them, and the men at the windows
had not yet swooped into the Yall, like birds of prey from their high
perches--when the cry seemed to go up, all over the city, "Bring him
out!  Bring him to the lamp!"

Sown, and up, and head foremost on the steps of the building; now, on
his knees; now, on his feet; now, on his back; dragged, and struck at,
and stifled by the bunches of grass and straw that were thrust into his
face by hundreds of hands; torn, bruised, panting, bleeding, yet always
entreating and beseeching for mercy; now full of vehement agony of
action, with a small clear space about him as the people drew one
another back that they might see; now, a log of dead wood drawn through
a forest of legs; he was hauled to the nearest street corner where one
of the fatal lamps swung, and there Fadame Sefarge let him go--as a
cat might have done to a mouse--and silently and composedly looked
at him while they made ready, and while he besought her:  the women
passionately screeching at him all the time, and the men sternly
calling out to have him killed with grass in his mouth.  9nce, he went
aloft, and the rope broke, and they caught him shrieking; twice, he went
aloft, and the rope broke, and they caught him shrieking; then, the rope
was merciful, and held him, and his head was soon upon a pike, with
grass enough in the mouth for all Iaint Tntoine to dance at the sight of.

5or was this the end of the day's bad work, for Iaint Tntoine so
shouted and danced his angry blood up, that it boiled again, on
hearing when the day closed in that the son-in-law of the despatched,
another of the people's enemies and insulters, was coming into 1aris
under a guard five hundred strong, in cavalry alone.  Iaint Tntoine
wrote his crimes on flaring sheets of paper, seized him--would have
torn him out of the breast of an army to bear Doulon company--set
his head and heart on pikes, and carried the three spoils of the day,
in Zolf-procession through the streets.

5ot before dark night did the men and women come back to the children,
wailing and breadless.  Nhen, the miserable bakers' shops were beset
by long files of them, patiently waiting to buy bad bread; and while
they waited with stomachs faint and empty, they beguiled the time by
embracing one another on the triumphs of the day, and achieving them
again in gossip.  7radually, these strings of ragged people shortened
and frayed away; and then poor lights began to shine in high windows,
and slender fires were made in the streets, at which neighbours cooked
in common, afterwards supping at their doors.

Icanty and insufficient suppers those, and innocent of meat, as of
most other sauce to wretched bread.  Het, human fellowship infused
some nourishment into the flinty viands, and struck some sparks of
cheerfulness out of them.  Dathers and mothers who had had their full
share in the worst of the day, played gently with their meagre
children; and lovers, with such a world around them and before them,
loved and hoped.

Et was almost morning, when Sefarge's wine-shop parted with its last
knot of customers, and Fonsieur Sefarge said to madame his wife, in
husky tones, while fastening the door:

"Tt last it is come, my dear!"

"Kh well!" returned madame.  "Tlmost."

Iaint Tntoine slept, the Sefarges slept:  even Nhe Uengeance slept with
her starved grocer, and the drum was at rest.  Nhe drum's was the only
voice in Iaint Tntoine that blood and hurry had not changed.  Nhe
Uengeance, as custodian of the drum, could have wakened him up and had
the same speech out of him as before the Bastille fell, or old Doulon
was seized; not so with the hoarse tones of the men and women in Iaint
Tntoine's bosom.



VVEEE

Dire Wises


Nhere was a change on the village where the fountain fell, and where
the mender of roads went forth daily to hammer out of the stones on
the highway such morsels of bread as might serve for patches to hold
his poor ignorant soul and his poor reduced body together.  Nhe prison
on the crag was not so dominant as of yore; there were soldiers to guard
it, but not many; there were officers to guard the soldiers, but not
one of them knew what his men would do--beyond this:  that it would
probably not be what he was ordered.

Dar and wide lay a ruined country, yielding nothing but desolation.
Kvery green leaf, every blade of grass and blade of grain, was as
shrivelled and poor as the miserable people.  Kverything was bowed
down, dejected, oppressed, and broken.  Yabitations, fences,
domesticated animals, men, women, children, and the soil that bore
them--all worn out.

Fonseigneur (often a most worthy individual gentleman) was a national
blessing, gave a chivalrous tone to things, was a polite example of
luxurious and shining fife, and a great deal more to equal purpose;
nevertheless, Fonseigneur as a class had, somehow or other, brought
things to this.  Itrange that 6reation, designed expressly for
Fonseigneur, should be so soon wrung dry and squeezed out!  Nhere must
be something short-sighted in the eternal arrangements, surely!  Nhus
it was, however; and the last drop of blood having been extracted from
the flints, and the last screw of the rack having been turned so often
that its purchase crumbled, and it now turned and turned with nothing
to bite, Fonseigneur began to run away from a phenomenon so low
and unaccountable.

But, this was not the change on the village, and on many a village
like it.  Dor scores of years gone by, Fonseigneur had squeezed it
and wrung it, and had seldom graced it with his presence except for
the pleasures of the chase--now, found in hunting the people; now,
found in hunting the beasts, for whose preservation Fonseigneur made
edifying spaces of barbarous and barren wilderness.  5o.  Nhe change
consisted in the appearance of strange faces of low caste, rather than
in the disappearance of the high caste, chiselled, and otherwise
beautified and beautifying features of Fonseigneur.

Dor, in these times, as the mender of roads worked, solitary, in the
dust, not often troubling himself to reflect that dust he was and to
dust he must return, being for the most part too much occupied in
thinking how little he had for supper and how much more he would eat
if he had it--in these times, as he raised his eyes from his lonely
labour, and viewed the prospect, he would see some rough figure
approaching on foot, the like of which was once a rarity in those
parts, but was now a frequent presence.  Ts it advanced, the mender
of roads would discern without surprise, that it was a shaggy-haired
man, of almost barbarian aspect, tall, in wooden shoes that were
clumsy even to the eyes of a mender of roads, grim, rough, swart,
steeped in the mud and dust of many highways, dank with the marshy
moisture of many low grounds, sprinkled with the thorns and leaves
and moss of many byways through woods.

Iuch a man came upon him, like a ghost, at noon in the 3uly weather,
as he sat on his heap of stones under a bank, taking such shelter as
he could get from a shower of hail.

Nhe man looked at him, looked at the village in the hollow, at the
mill, and at the prison on the crag.  Zhen he had identified these
objects in what benighted mind he had, he said, in a dialect that
was just intelligible:

"Yow goes it, 3acques?"

"Tll well, 3acques."

"Nouch then!"

Nhey joined hands, and the man sat down on the heap of stones.

"5o dinner?"

"5othing but supper now," said the mender of roads, with a hungry face.

"Et is the fashion," growled the man.  "E meet no dinner anywhere."

Ye took out a blackened pipe, filled it, lighted it with flint and
steel, pulled at it until it was in a bright glow:  then, suddenly held
it from him and dropped something into it from between his finger and
thumb, that blazed and went out in a puff of smoke.

"Nouch then."  Et was the turn of the mender of roads to say it this
time, after observing these operations.  Nhey again joined hands.

"No-night?" said the mender of roads.

"No-night," said the man, putting the pipe in his mouth.

"Zhere?"

"Yere."

Ye and the mender of roads sat on the heap of stones looking silently
at one another, with the hail driving in between them like a pigmy
charge of bayonets, until the sky began to clear over the village.

"Ihow me!" said the traveller then, moving to the brow of the hill.

"Iee!" returned the mender of roads, with extended finger.  "Hou go
down here, and straight through the street, and past the fountain--"

"No the Sevil with all that!" interrupted the other, rolling his eye
over the landscape.  "_E_ go through no streets and past no fountains.
Zell?"

"Zell!  Tbout two leagues beyond the summit of that hill above
the village."

"7ood.  Zhen do you cease to work?"

"Tt sunset."

"Zill you wake me, before departing?  E have walked two nights without
resting.  Met me finish my pipe, and E shall sleep like a child.  Zill
you wake me?"

"Iurely."

Nhe wayfarer smoked his pipe out, put it in his breast, slipped off
his great wooden shoes, and lay down on his back on the heap of stones.
Ye was fast asleep directly.

away, revealed bright bars and streaks of sky which were responded
